Platform Requirements

Balance Compare & Rebalance

Defines how the platform keeps a student's Pay Over Time plan aligned with their current institutional balance — spanning the initial comparison that drives enrollment decisions, parallel-agreement scenarios when multiple plans coexist, and the nightly rebalance engine that redistributes remaining installments after balance changes.

Configuration Philosophy

Institution Admin / NBS Implementation Engineer

Single Source of Balance Truth. The platform must treat the institutional ERP ledger as the authoritative source for a student's current balance. All balance compare calculations, rebalance triggers, and plan amount adjustments must derive from the ERP-posted balance, not from a cached or stale snapshot. Institutions must be able to configure how frequently the ERP feed reconciles (minimum: daily; recommended: real-time webhook on charge or credit posting).

Why: Plans built on stale balances create reconciliation failures, student confusion, and institution write-offs. A contract-based integration with a definitive refresh cadence gives the institution confidence that plan amounts track the actual receivable.

Institution Admin

Balance Compare Tolerance Band. Institutions must be able to configure an acceptable tolerance (default: $0; max: institution-defined dollar amount or percentage) below which a balance increase does not trigger a rebalance notification or plan adjustment. This prevents micro-adjustments from minor late-fee postings disrupting consumer experience while still surfacing material balance changes.

Why: A $0.50 rounding difference should not generate a balance-change alert card and force a consumer decision. Configurable tolerance lets the institution tune sensitivity to their fee-posting patterns.

Phase 1 — Standard Balance Compare

Student / AP

Eligible Balance Displayed at Enrollment. During plan enrollment the system must display the student's full current eligible balance (charges within the configured enrollment window; excluded charge types removed per institution config). The displayed amount must match the ERP ledger balance at the moment the consumer reaches the enrollment step, and the plan must be built on that amount. If the balance changes between page load and plan submission, the system must refresh and present the updated amount before allowing confirmation.

Why: TILA disclosure accuracy requires the plan amount at confirmation to reflect the actual balance being financed. A stale amount at confirmation invalidates the disclosure.

Student / AP / Institution Finance Admin

Post-Enrollment Balance Change Detection. After a plan is active, the system must detect changes to the student's institutional balance (new charges, financial aid credits, corrections) and compare them against the plan's covered amount. Changes exceeding the configured tolerance band must surface as a balance-change alert on the consumer's home screen within one business day of the ERP posting. The alert must show the prior plan amount, the new balance, and the difference.

Why: Consumers need to understand when their plan no longer covers their full balance so they can decide to adjust, make an additional payment, or contact the bursar — before late fees accumulate on uncovered charges.

Student / AP

Consumer Adjustment Preference. When a balance increase is detected, the system must present the consumer with a choice: (a) Increase plan to cover the new balance — remaining installments are recalculated evenly; (b) Leave plan as-is and be prompted to pay the difference separately; (c) Contact the bursar. The consumer's selection must be recorded and surfaced to institution users in the plan management view.

Why: Not all balance increases represent charges the student agrees with. Forcing automatic plan expansion would enroll the student in a larger obligation without consent, creating regulatory and trust risk.

Phase 2 — Parallel Agreements

Institution Admin

Parallel Agreement Enablement. Institutions must be able to configure whether a student may hold more than one active plan simultaneously (e.g., a Fall Standard plan and a Spring Standard plan co-existing). When parallel agreements are enabled, the system must track each plan's covered balance, installment schedule, and payment method independently. Balance compare must run independently against each plan's covered charges rather than aggregating all plans against the total balance.

Why: Multi-term enrollment is common; students who set up a Fall plan in August should not be required to cancel it when enrolling a Spring plan. Parallel agreements eliminate the friction of sequential plan management.

Student / AP

Multi-Plan Home Screen View. When a student has two or more active plans, the home screen must display each plan as a separate card with its own next payment date, amount, remaining installments, and covered term label. The combined total of next scheduled payments across all plans must be surfaced prominently so the consumer understands total upcoming auto-debit exposure.

Why: A student with two plans for two terms may owe two auto-debits in the same week. Surfacing the aggregate exposure prevents surprise NSF situations and builds consumer trust.

Student / AP

Start Date Configuration for Future-Term Plans. When parallel agreements are enabled, institutions must be able to configure a plan start date offset (e.g., "plan begins 30 days before term start") so that future-term plans are created but no payments process until the configured start date. Consumers enrolling in a future plan must see the first payment date clearly in the confirmation and TILA disclosure.

Why: Charging a student for a Spring semester plan in October before any Spring charges are due creates consumer dissatisfaction and potential refund requests. Start date configuration aligns first payments with actual charge due dates.

Phase 3 — Financial Responsibility Model

Institution Admin / NBS Implementation Engineer

Financial Responsibility Agreement Linkage. In Phase 3, institutions must be able to configure the platform to require acceptance of an updated Financial Responsibility Agreement (FRA) when a student's plan amount increases by more than a configured threshold. The FRA acceptance must be recorded with a timestamp, the accepted document version, and the student's authentication state at time of acceptance. The signed FRA must be retrievable via the correspondence history and the reporting API.

Why: Consumer protection law and institutional policy increasingly require that students formally re-acknowledge financial obligations when those obligations increase materially. Embedding FRA acceptance in the balance-increase flow ensures compliance at the moment of consent.

Institution Finance Admin / NBS PAS Support Agent

Financial Responsibility Audit Trail. The platform must maintain a tamper-evident, append-only audit record for every FRA acceptance event: student identifier, document version hash, acceptance timestamp, IP address, device fingerprint, and authentication method used. This record must be exportable in the Plan Agreement report (see Report URS) and retained for a minimum of 7 years or the institution's configured retention period, whichever is longer.

Why: If a student disputes a charge or challenges their legal obligation, the institution needs incontrovertible proof of what was agreed to, when, and by whom. A cryptographically tamper-evident log meets the evidentiary standard for collections and legal proceedings.

Rebalance Rules & Eligibility

Student / AP

Nightly Automatic Rebalance. The platform must run a nightly rebalance job that evaluates every active plan against the student's current ERP balance. Where the ERP balance has changed and the consumer has elected to keep the plan covering their full balance (or the institution has auto-rebalance enabled), the remaining installments must be redistributed evenly across the remaining payment dates. The first rebalanced installment amount is effective the following business day.

Why: Nightly rebalance keeps plan amounts current without requiring consumer action for every minor charge change, reducing service calls and improving reconciliation accuracy.

Student / AP

10-Day Proximity Rule. The nightly rebalance engine must not increase the amount of any installment whose scheduled date is within 10 calendar days of the rebalance run date. If a rebalance would require increasing a near-term installment, that amount must roll forward to the next eligible installment (more than 10 days out) and the near-term installment remains at its previously scheduled amount. If no future installment exists beyond the 10-day window, the increase must be surfaced as an uncovered balance alert rather than processed automatically.

Why: Increasing an installment 2 days before it processes gives the consumer no time to ensure funds are available, leading to NSF fees, returned payments, and plan cancellation. The 10-day rule protects the consumer and reduces failed payment rates.

Student / AP

Decrease Rebalance Always Applies. The 10-day proximity protection applies only to increases. If a credit posts (financial aid disbursement, scholarship, fee waiver) that reduces the covered balance, the rebalance engine must apply the decrease to the next installment regardless of proximity, never holding a consumer to a higher payment than their actual balance requires.

Why: Holding a consumer to an elevated payment after aid is posted — even temporarily — creates an overpayment situation and negative consumer sentiment. Decreases should always benefit the consumer immediately.

Institution Finance Admin

Rebalance On Login. In addition to nightly rebalance, the platform must trigger a real-time balance compare whenever a student or AP authenticates. If the ERP balance has changed since the last comparison and the delta exceeds the configured tolerance, the home screen must show the balance-change alert card immediately on that session. This supplements the nightly job to catch same-day charge postings.

Why: A student who logs in to make a payment shouldn't see a plan amount that doesn't match their balance. On-login compare closes the gap between nightly runs and real-time activity.

Platform Requirements

Payment Allocation

Defines the rules by which incoming payments are applied to a student's outstanding charges — covering default priority ordering, consumer choices, CSR override authority, guarantee plan specifics, and institution-level configuration options. Allocation rules affect downstream ERP write-back, reconciliation accuracy, and consumer trust.

Default Allocation Priority

Platform / Institution Finance Admin

Past-Due First Allocation. The platform default must apply incoming payments to the oldest past-due charges first (earliest due date, ascending). Within the same due date, the platform must apply to the highest-dollar charge first. This ordering must apply to all payment types — one-time payments, plan installments, and guest payments — unless overridden by institution configuration or consumer election.

Why: Past-due balances accrue late fees and trigger registration holds first. Applying payments to them first reduces the consumer's ongoing financial penalty and aligns with common institutional collections policy.

Platform / Institution Finance Admin

Secondary Allocation for Remaining Funds. After past-due charges are satisfied, remaining payment funds must be applied to current-term charges in due-date ascending order. Where current-term charges share a due date (e.g., tuition and housing both due the same day), funds must be distributed proportionally across charge types unless the institution configures a charge-type priority sequence.

Why: Proportional allocation within the same due date avoids arbitrarily leaving one charge type unpaid when funds are sufficient to cover most but not all of the due date's total, which would create partial holds and student confusion.

Institution Finance Admin

Charge-Type Priority Configuration. Institution admins must be able to configure a ranked charge-type priority list (e.g., 1. Tuition, 2. Housing, 3. Fees, 4. Other) that overrides proportional distribution within the same due date. This configuration must be term-agnostic (applies to all terms) and must take effect within one business day of being saved.

Why: Some institutions have regulatory or policy requirements to satisfy tuition obligations before housing or vice versa. Configurable priority respects institutional policy without requiring custom code.

Consumer-Driven Allocation

Student / AP

Consumer Allocation Election for One-Time Payments. When the institution has enabled consumer allocation choice (off by default), a student or AP making a one-time payment must be presented with an allocation selector showing all eligible outstanding charge buckets (e.g., Fall 2025 Tuition $3,200, Spring 2026 Housing $2,400). The consumer may either accept the default allocation or drag-rank or enter amounts per bucket. The elected allocation must persist in the payment receipt and ERP write-back.

Why: Some consumers have strategic reasons to pay specific charges (e.g., paying housing to avoid eviction while deferring tuition under a payment plan). Enabling allocation choice for those institutions reduces escalation calls and consumer dissatisfaction.

Student / AP

Plan Installment Allocation is Fixed. Plan installments must always allocate according to the platform default (past-due first, then pro-rated current charges) regardless of the consumer allocation election setting. Consumers may not redirect plan installments to specific charge types. This is a non-configurable rule because the plan agreement specifies a covered balance, and allowing redirection would break the covered-balance guarantee.

Why: The plan agreement is a TILA-covered contract. Permitting consumers to redirect installment allocation post-agreement would create a mismatch between what was disclosed and what was applied, creating legal risk.

CSR Override Capabilities

Institution Finance Admin / NBS PAS Support Agent

Manual Allocation Override. Authorized institution users (Finance Admin and above) and NBS PAS Support Agents must be able to manually re-allocate a recently processed payment (within 5 business days of processing) to different charge buckets. The override must require a written reason, must be recorded in the payment audit trail, and must trigger an updated payment confirmation to the consumer and institution admin. The ERP write-back must reflect the corrected allocation.

Why: Payment posting errors occur — financial aid disbursements land on wrong terms, charges are miscoded. CSRs need a correction path that doesn't require payment reversal when only the allocation was wrong.

NBS PAS Support Agent

Allocation Override Audit Trail. Every manual allocation override must generate an immutable audit record containing: the original allocation, the corrected allocation, the authorizing user identifier, the timestamp, the stated reason, and the ERP write-back status. This record must be visible in the plan/payment management view to all users with Finance Admin access or higher and to NBS PAS Support Agents.

Why: Allocation overrides touch financial records. A tamper-evident audit trail is required for compliance, dispute resolution, and external audit.

Guarantee Plan Allocation

Platform / Institution Finance Admin

Guarantee Plan — State-Defined Allocation. For Guarantee Plans (regulated in states with guarantee plan statutes), the platform must apply the allocation sequence mandated by the applicable state regulation, which may differ from the platform default. The system must maintain a configuration table mapping state codes to their required allocation sequences and must apply the correct sequence based on the institution's state of domicile and the student's state of residence where relevant.

Why: Guarantee plan statutes in states like California (TICAS) define specific allocation requirements. Deviating from these is a regulatory violation. A configuration table is more maintainable than hardcoded logic as regulations evolve.

Institution Finance Admin

Guarantee Plan — Non-Redistributable Allocation. For Guarantee Plans, once an installment is applied to a specific charge, the allocation must not be subject to consumer election or CSR manual override. Corrections must go through a formal reversal and re-posting workflow that creates a complete audit trail and notifies the relevant state authority if required by the applicable statute.

Why: Guarantee plan statutes typically prohibit informal reallocation because the guarantee is tied to specific covered charges. Breaking the allocation chain invalidates the guarantee.

Overpayment & Edge Cases

Student / AP

Overpayment Handling. If an incoming payment exceeds the student's total outstanding balance, the system must automatically create a credit balance on the student's account. The institution must be able to configure the default disposition of credit balances: (a) hold for future charges, (b) automatically initiate a refund request after X days, or (c) apply to the next term's charges if available. The consumer must be notified of the credit balance via their preferred contact method within one business day of the overpayment being detected.

Why: Overpayments happen when financial aid arrives while a payment is in transit. Automatic credit handling and consumer notification prevents the consumer from unknowingly having funds tied up at the institution.

Institution Finance Admin

Partial Payment Minimum Threshold. Institutions must be able to configure a minimum one-time payment amount (default: $1.00). Payments below the configured minimum must be rejected at the payment step with a clear error message showing the minimum. This configuration does not apply to plan installments (which are system-calculated) but does apply to consumer-initiated one-time payments and guest payments.

Why: Processing fees on sub-threshold payments often exceed the payment amount itself, creating net-negative transactions. A configurable minimum protects the institution's payment processing economics.

Institution Configuration

Institution Admin / NBS Implementation Engineer

Allocation Rule Versioning. Any change to an institution's allocation configuration (charge-type priority, consumer election toggle, credit balance disposition, minimum threshold) must create a new versioned configuration record with an effective date. The system must apply the allocation rules that were active at the time the payment was initiated, not the current configuration. Historic allocation records must reference the configuration version that governed them.

Why: Institutions change allocation rules between terms. A payment from October processed under October rules should not be retroactively re-evaluated under January rules. Version-locked configuration ensures consistent, auditable allocation history.

Platform Requirements

Plan Offer Configuration

Defines how institutions configure the plan offers presented to consumers during Pay Over Time enrollment — covering plan type selection, eligibility and enrollment windows, schedule and payment settings, how offers are surfaced to consumers, fee rules, and access rules for students and authorized parties.

Plan Type & Identity

Institution Admin / NBS Implementation Engineer

Plan Offer Creation. Institution admins must be able to create named plan offers within their account. Each offer must have: a unique internal identifier (system-generated), a consumer-facing display name (institution-defined, e.g., "Fall 2025 Standard Payment Plan"), a plan type (Standard, Past Due, Long-Term, International, Guarantee, Hidden, Custom), a term association (or "rolling" for non-term plans), and an active/inactive status. Inactive offers are not shown to consumers but retain historical enrollment records.

Why: Institutions run multiple plan offers simultaneously — different plans for different student populations, terms, or charge types. A named, typed, term-associated offer model supports this complexity without requiring separate system configurations per plan.

Institution Admin

Plan Type Constraints. The system must enforce plan type combination rules at the offer-configuration level: (a) Standard plans and Guarantee plans cannot be offered simultaneously for the same term (mutually exclusive families); (b) Hidden plans are always type Standard family but require an explicit "invite-only" flag; (c) Custom plans must define all schedule parameters at enrollment time rather than inheriting defaults. The UI must surface conflict warnings when an admin attempts to create a combination that violates these rules.

Why: Misconfigured plan combinations confuse consumers and create billing errors. Enforcing type constraints at configuration time prevents downstream enrollment problems.

Eligibility & Enrollment Windows

Institution Admin

Enrollment Window Configuration. Each plan offer must have a configurable enrollment window defined by an open date and a close date (or "close X days before term start"). The window governs when consumers can initiate enrollment. Outside the window, the plan offer must not appear in the consumer enrollment flow (not hidden after close — simply unavailable). Admins must be able to extend or close a window manually regardless of the configured dates.

Why: Enrollment windows protect the institution's billing cycle — allowing late plan enrollment after billing has run creates reconciliation complexity. Configurable windows let institutions align plan availability with their operational calendar.

Institution Admin

Eligibility Rule Configuration. Institutions must be able to configure eligibility rules for each plan offer. Supported rule types must include: minimum balance required, maximum balance allowed, enrollment status (active enrollment required, not required, or past-due-only), student standing (good standing, any standing, financial hold students excluded), and program/campus filter. Students who do not meet all eligibility rules must not be shown the plan offer, with a configurable reason message displayed instead.

Why: Not all plans are appropriate for all students. A past-due recovery plan should not be offered to a student in good standing with a current-term balance. Eligibility rules ensure the right offer reaches the right student population.

Institution Admin

Maximum Enrollment Cap. Institution admins must be able to set a maximum enrollment count per plan offer. When the cap is reached, the plan offer must automatically become unavailable to new enrollees. The admin must receive a notification when enrollment reaches 80% and 100% of the cap. The cap must be editable at any time; increasing the cap immediately re-opens enrollment if the offer is within its enrollment window.

Why: Some institutions limit enrollment in specialized plans (e.g., a small pilot program) for operational reasons. An enrollment cap with proactive notification lets admins manage capacity without manual monitoring.

Schedule & Payment Settings

Institution Admin

Installment Count Options. For Standard and Guarantee plan offers, institutions must be able to configure which installment count options are presented to consumers (e.g., 3, 4, 5, 6 installments). At least one option must be selected; a maximum of 6 options may be offered simultaneously. The default option (pre-selected in the Quick Pick UI) must be designatable by the admin. For Long-Term (LTPP) plans, the configurable range is 3–24 months.

Why: Installment options drive both consumer affordability perception and institution cash-flow planning. Configurable options let institutions balance consumer choice against operational complexity.

Institution Admin

Payment Day Configuration. Institutions must be able to configure the day of month on which plan installments process (e.g., always the 1st, always the 15th, or consumer-selectable from a configured list of days). Where consumer selection is enabled, the consumer must choose their preferred payment day during enrollment. The first installment date must respect the plan's start date and the selected payment day, and must be surfaced clearly in the TILA pre-disclosure before confirmation.

Why: Consumers are more likely to maintain sufficient funds on a payment date they chose that aligns with their pay cycle. Consumer-selectable payment days reduce NSF rates.

Institution Admin

Required Full Eligible Balance Enrollment. Institutions must be able to configure whether consumers may enroll in a plan for a partial balance (amount less than eligible balance) or whether the plan must cover the full eligible balance. When "full balance required" is enabled, the enrollment flow must not allow the consumer to modify the plan amount downward from the calculated eligible balance. When partial balance is allowed, a minimum plan amount (dollar or percentage of eligible balance) must be configurable.

Why: Some institutions require full-balance plans to prevent students from using a small plan to obtain enrollment clearance while leaving most of their balance unpaid. Full-balance enforcement is a collections risk management tool.

Consumer Offer Presentation

Student / AP

Quick Pick Offer Surface. When exactly one plan offer is available to a consumer, or when an institution has designated a "featured" offer, the enrollment flow must present a Quick Pick card showing: the plan name, number of installments, first payment date, installment amount, and total plan amount. The consumer may confirm the Quick Pick directly (one click) or tap "Customize" to adjust installment count or payment day within the configured allowed options.

Why: Research shows that presenting a clear, low-friction default dramatically increases plan enrollment conversion. Quick Pick reduces cognitive load for the most common case while preserving consumer control.

Student / AP

Multi-Offer Selection. When more than one plan offer is available to a consumer, the enrollment flow must display all eligible offers as selectable cards, each showing plan type, installment count options, first payment estimate, and any applicable enrollment fee. The consumer must select exactly one offer before proceeding. Offers must be sorted by the institution-configured display order (default: alphabetical by plan name).

Why: Presenting multiple offers simultaneously with a clear comparison surface lets the consumer make an informed choice without requiring them to navigate back and forth between offer detail pages.

Fees & Completion Rules

Institution Admin

Enrollment Fee Configuration. Institutions must be able to configure an enrollment fee per plan offer (flat dollar amount, 0 to institution-defined max). The fee must be: disclosed in the Quick Pick card and the TILA pre-disclosure, charged at the time of enrollment confirmation (not included in installments), non-refundable unless the institution configures a refund window, and written back to the ERP as a separate fee line item distinct from the covered balance installments.

Why: Enrollment fees are a common institution revenue source that offset payment plan administration costs. Keeping the fee separate from installments preserves allocation transparency and simplifies TILA disclosure math.

Institution Admin

Plan Completion Notification. When all installments on a plan have successfully processed and the covered balance reaches zero, the system must automatically send a plan completion notification to the consumer (email and optionally SMS) and update the student's account status in the ERP to reflect the plan as complete. Institution admins must be able to configure the completion notification template and toggle the ERP status update on or off.

Why: Plan completion is a significant positive milestone for the student. A confirmation notification reinforces trust and provides a receipt artifact for the student's records. The ERP status update enables downstream processes (registration clearance, diploma release) that depend on financial standing.

Plan Accessibility — Student & AP

Student / AP

AP Enrollment Authority. Institution admins must be able to configure whether Authorized Parties may enroll in a plan on behalf of a student (default: allowed for plan owners, not allowed for view-only APs). When AP enrollment is allowed, the plan owner designation must be set at enrollment time: (a) student is plan owner and AP is listed as authorized payer, or (b) AP is plan owner with autopay drawn from AP's payment method. The plan detail view must clearly identify the plan owner and the payment method owner.

Why: Parents frequently set up payment plans for their children. Enabling AP-initiated enrollment with clear ownership attribution reduces service calls and ensures the correct party's payment method is charged.

Institution Admin

Hidden Plan Invite Distribution. For plan offers configured as Hidden (invite-only), institution admins must be able to distribute invites via: (a) bulk upload of student ID list (CSV), (b) individual student lookup and invite, or (c) rule-based auto-invite (e.g., all students with a past-due balance over $500). Invited students must receive an email containing a direct enrollment link that bypasses the standard plan-selection step and routes directly to the specific hidden plan's enrollment flow. Invite links must expire after the configured enrollment window closes.

Why: Hidden plans are used for targeted populations — past-due recovery cohorts, scholarship recipients, pilot groups. Bulk invite and rule-based distribution eliminates manual individual outreach while the expiring link ensures the offer is time-bounded.

Balance Amount Plan Parameters

Deferred Feature Not in scope for initial launch. Flagged for future roadmap prioritization. These requirements define the configuration model for balance-tier-based plan parameters; schedule for review after Phase 1 URS and Plan Offer Configuration are validated in production.

Institution Admin / Finance Admin

Balance Tier Threshold Configuration. Institution admins can define balance tier thresholds and assign a distinct installment count range and enrollment fee to each tier (e.g., balances under $500 → max 3 installments; $500–$2,000 → max 6; $2,000+ → max 12). Each tier's minimum and maximum installment count and enrollment fee are independently configurable.

Why: Institutions need to manage financial risk and servicing cost proportional to balance size; applying the same plan parameters to a $200 balance and a $5,000 balance creates unnecessary exposure at the low end and inadequate flexibility at the high end.

Student / Consumer

Automatic Tier Selection at Enrollment. At the time a student initiates plan enrollment, the system evaluates the student's current eligible balance and automatically applies the parameter set associated with the matching tier. The student is never required to select a tier manually.

Why: Manual tier selection introduces friction and creates support burden; automatic selection ensures accurate plan configuration without requiring the consumer to understand institution-internal rules.

Institution Admin / Finance Admin

Tier Configuration Version Control. Changes to tier thresholds, installment count ranges, or enrollment fees are effective on a future date specified by the admin. Changes do not apply retroactively to plans already enrolled under the prior configuration. The system retains a history of all tier configuration versions with effective dates and the identity of the admin who made each change.

Why: Retroactive parameter changes would invalidate enrollment agreements already accepted by students; versioning protects both the institution's contractual obligations and the student's plan terms as agreed at enrollment.

Platform Requirements

Platform Requirements

Payment Processing

Core payment processing capabilities, supported methods, partner-specific rules, settlement patterns, and failure handling. These requirements cover everything from method acceptance through reconciliation back to the institution.

Accepted Payment Methods

As a student / authorized party

ACH / eCheck. The platform must support ACH debit as the primary payment method for payment plans and one-time account payments. Students must be able to save one or more bank accounts and designate a default for auto-debit. ACH must comply with NACHA rules including authorization language, return code handling, and re-presentment limits.

Why: ACH is the lowest-cost processing method for both institution and student. Ensuring NACHA compliance protects the institution from regulatory risk and reduces the volume of disputed or unauthorized returns.

As a student / authorized party

Credit & Debit Card. The platform must accept Visa, Mastercard, Discover, and American Express for one-time payments. Card acceptance for payment plan enrollment is configurable per institution — some institutions disable card payment on plans to avoid interchange costs. All card transactions must be PCI-DSS compliant and processed through a tokenized card vault; raw PAN data must never touch Horizon application servers.

Why: Some students and families prefer or require card payment for one-time charges. Tokenization is mandatory to protect the institution from PCI scope and liability. Institution-level control over card-on-plan acceptance lets schools manage their processing cost structure.

As an institution admin

Convenience Fee Configuration. The platform must support institution-configured convenience fees for credit/debit card payments. Fee structures may be flat per transaction, percentage-based, or a combination. Fee disclosure must appear at the point of card entry, prior to payment confirmation, and must satisfy applicable state disclosure requirements.

Why: Card interchange costs are significant at scale. Institutions need to recover these costs via convenience fees rather than subsidizing card-paying students with tuition rate increases that affect all students.

Partner Exclusions & Special Processing

Existing Institution Payment Processor Relationships. Some NCC client institutions have pre-existing payment processor contracts (e.g., with TouchNet, CashNet, or other campus gateway providers) that predate their Horizon contract. The platform architecture must define how Horizon integrates with or routes around these existing gateways. In cases where the institution requires payment processing to flow through their existing gateway, Horizon must support a pass-through or redirect model without degrading the consumer experience.

Why: Requiring an institution to terminate an existing payment processor contract to adopt Horizon is a significant adoption barrier. A gateway-agnostic payment acceptance layer protects sales velocity and reduces institutional friction during implementation.

International Payment Vendor Routing. Institutions with configured international payment plans must route international student payments through the designated international payment vendor (Flywire, Convera, Western Union Education, etc.) rather than through standard ACH/card processing. The routing logic must be based on student enrollment classification or self-selection at point of payment, not country of origin of the card or bank account.

Why: International students may have US bank accounts yet still owe charges denominated in a specific currency arrangement. Routing logic based on enrollment classification (F-1 visa status, for example) is more reliable than payment instrument origin.

Guarantee Plan Settlement Exclusion. Payments on Guarantee Plans are settled differently from standard payment plan payments — the institution receives guaranteed disbursements from NBS on a fixed schedule regardless of whether the student pays. Student payments on Guarantee Plans are remitted to NBS, not directly to the institution. The platform must prevent Guarantee Plan payment transactions from being included in institution settlement batches and must route them through the correct NBS settlement path.

Why: Incorrect settlement routing on Guarantee Plans would result in double-payment to the institution and revenue recognition errors on the NBS side. This is a compliance and financial controls requirement, not just a UX one.

Settlement & Reconciliation

As an institution finance admin

Daily Settlement Reporting. The platform must produce a daily settlement report detailing all payments processed, their amounts, payment methods, and the net amount being deposited to the institution. Settlement reports must be available by the start of business the following banking day and must be exportable in formats compatible with common higher ed ERP systems (CSV, NACHA, XML).

Why: Institution finance teams need daily reconciliation to match Horizon settlements against their general ledger entries. Delays or format mismatches in settlement reporting create manual reconciliation burdens that erode satisfaction.

Payment Application & ERP Write-back. When a payment is processed, the platform must send a payment application record back to the institution's ERP/SIS within the configured sync window (real-time or batch). The write-back must reduce the outstanding student account balance in the SIS so that the institution's internal records stay current and financial holds can be lifted promptly when balances clear.

Why: The original receivable at the institution remains outstanding when a student is on a Horizon payment plan (unlike a loan model). This makes accurate, timely payment application back to the SIS critical — otherwise institution staff see stale balances and may incorrectly apply holds or withhold transcripts from students who are actively paying.

Failure Handling & Retry

As a student

Payment Failure Notification. When a scheduled payment fails (NSF, account closed, authorization error), the platform must notify the student via email and in-app notification within 24 hours. The notification must clearly state the reason (if available), the amount that failed, the next scheduled payment date, and the steps to cure the failure before the plan is placed in jeopardy.

Why: Students who aren't notified promptly about payment failures are more likely to end up in default through inaction rather than through inability to pay. Early, clear notification increases recovery rates and reduces the number of plans that escalate to termination.

Retry Configuration. The platform must support institution-configurable retry rules for failed payments: number of retry attempts (0–3), days between retries, and whether to retry on a different saved payment method if available. The default configuration must comply with NACHA rules (maximum 2 re-presentments for ACH returns). Retry attempts must be clearly reflected in the student's account activity and disclosed in the payment plan agreement.

Why: Exceeding NACHA re-presentment limits creates legal exposure. Institution-configurable retries let schools balance recovery rates against the relationship risk of repeatedly attempting a payment the student cannot cover.

Platform Requirements

School Structure Setup

How institutions are modeled within Horizon — tenancy, payment spaces, multi-campus configurations, and the relationship between organizational structure and product configuration.

Tenancy Model

Single-Tenant Institution Isolation. Each NCC client institution must exist as a fully isolated tenant within Horizon. Student data, payment configurations, plan templates, correspondence templates, and admin user access must be completely segregated by tenant. No student data, report data, or configuration from one institution should be accessible, queryable, or visible to any other institution's tenant — including NCC staff — except through explicitly permissioned reporting and support tooling.

Why: FERPA requires strict control over student financial data. A multi-tenant data leak — even in a reporting or admin interface — creates regulatory exposure and could terminate the institution relationship. Isolation must be enforced at the data layer, not just the UI layer.

Multi-System Institution Support. Some institutions operate multiple ERP/SIS instances — for example, a university system where undergraduate, graduate, and law school students exist in separate Banner or Colleague instances. The platform must support a single Horizon tenant mapping to multiple ERP instances, with student accounts resolved to the correct ERP at the point of payment application. Configuration must allow different payment plan templates and billing rules per sub-system.

Why: Large state university systems and those that have grown through acquisition frequently run multiple SIS instances. Requiring separate Horizon contracts and tenants for each SIS instance would be a commercial and operational barrier — these institutions expect a unified view of their Horizon configuration.

Payment Spaces

Payment Space Definition. A Payment Space is a configurable sub-unit within an institution tenant that groups a set of charges, plan templates, and payment rules together. Examples: a university may have a "Tuition & Fees" payment space and a separate "Housing" payment space, each with different plan options, due dates, and settlement destinations. Students may have charges in one or both payment spaces simultaneously.

Why: Higher education billing is rarely monolithic. A student's tuition may be billed and collected by the registrar's office while housing is billed by residential life — each with distinct terms, late fee rules, and remediation paths. Payment Spaces model this reality without requiring multiple Horizon tenants or forcing an artificial merge of distinct billing streams.

As an institution admin

Payment Space Configuration. Institution admins must be able to configure which charge types (by ERP charge code) are included in each Payment Space, which plan templates are available in each Space, and whether a student must resolve charges in one Space before accessing another. Cross-space payment plans (a single plan that covers charges from multiple Payment Spaces) must also be supportable where the institution requires it.

Why: Without admin-controlled Payment Space configuration, NBS implementation teams would need to hard-code institutional billing logic at the customer level — a maintenance burden that doesn't scale as institutions' billing structures change each year.

Consumer-Facing Payment Space Presentation. When a student has charges in multiple Payment Spaces, the consumer UI must present each Space distinctly — labeled, with its own balance, plan options, and actions. The student should not need to understand the concept of "Payment Spaces" but must be able to clearly see that they have obligations in separate areas (e.g., "Tuition Balance" and "Housing Balance") and take action on each independently or together where possible.

Why: Students experiencing a confusingly merged balance view between fundamentally different charge types (tuition vs. housing, for example) will call the bursar. Clarity at the charge-group level reduces inbound support volume and improves self-service success rates.

Multi-Campus & Consortium Configurations

Campus-Level Configuration Inheritance. For multi-campus institutions, the platform must support a parent tenant with child campus units. Child campuses inherit configuration from the parent by default but can override specific settings (plan templates, correspondence, late fee rules) at the campus level. Admin access can be scoped to the parent (sees all campuses) or to a specific campus (sees only their students).

Why: State university systems contract at the system level but need campus autonomy for billing rules — a community college campus and a flagship research university within the same system have very different student populations and payment cultures. Inheritance with override supports both system-level consistency and campus-level flexibility.

Platform Requirements

Access Management

Role-based access control across institution admin users, NBS internal staff, students, and authorized parties. Defines the six institution role types, three NBS staff roles, ten permission-secured platform actions, and user provisioning requirements.

Institution Admin Roles

Full Admin. The Full Admin role is the institution-level super-user. A Full Admin has unrestricted access to every feature within the institution's Horizon tenant: configuration (payment plan setup, fee rules, term windows), user management (add/deactivate/modify all institution users and roles), student account management (view, adjust, override), reporting (all reports including audit log export), and payment operations (process payments, issue refunds, cancel plans). At least one Full Admin must be designated per tenant at go-live. Full Admin can delegate any subset of their capabilities to lower roles.

Why: Every institution needs a single owner-level account that can act on any situation without contacting NBS support. This is the role held by the Bursar Director or VP of Finance — the person who is ultimately accountable for the tenant's configuration and financial accuracy.

Finance Admin. The Finance Admin role covers all payment and financial operations but excludes system configuration changes and user management. Finance Admins can: process and reverse payments, issue refunds, enroll and cancel payment plans on behalf of students, generate and export all financial reports (daily activity, plan enrollment, reconciliation, audit log), and view complete student account history. Finance Admins cannot: modify plan templates, change fee rules, add or deactivate other users, or change institution-level configuration settings.

Why: At most institutions, the staff who process daily financial transactions are not the same people who configure the system. The Finance Admin role gives bursar cashiers and accounts receivable staff the operational access they need without exposing system configuration to change risk from high-turnover front-line staff.

Student Services Rep. The Student Services Rep role is scoped to student-account-level operations: view student balance and account history, view active and historical payment plans, make payments on behalf of a student (with student verification), create custom payment arrangements (subject to institutional policy configuration), and add notes to a student account. Student Services Reps cannot: generate financial reports, view aggregate institution-level data, issue refunds, cancel plans, or access system configuration. They have read access to the plan catalog to answer enrollment questions.

Why: Student services front desks handle a high volume of student-facing inquiries. They need enough account visibility to answer questions and take action without the ability to accidentally trigger financial operations (refunds, cancellations) that require supervisory review. The restriction on aggregate reporting is also a FERPA risk mitigation — student services reps should not be able to export bulk student data.

Reporting & Compliance Admin. The Reporting & Compliance Admin role provides read-only access to all reports and audit logs, including the Financial Responsibility Agreement completion report, audit log export, outstanding balance report, and term reconciliation report. This role cannot view individual student account details beyond what appears in report exports, cannot initiate any transactions, and cannot access system configuration. Designed for internal audit, compliance, and institutional research staff who need data visibility without operational access.

Why: Many institutions have compliance officers, internal auditors, or institutional research staff who need periodic access to Horizon data for audit, accreditation, or analysis purposes. Without a read-only reporting role, institutions are forced to either share Finance Admin credentials (a compliance violation) or route all data requests through bursar staff (an operational burden).

Payment Plan Coordinator. The Payment Plan Coordinator role is a specialized operations role focused entirely on payment plan lifecycle management. Coordinators can: enroll students in payment plans, modify active plan schedules (within configured rules), cancel plans with required documentation, view and respond to plan-related student communications, and generate plan-specific reports (enrollment, delinquency, upcoming payment). They cannot: process non-plan payments, issue refunds, access billing/statement presentment, or modify plan configuration templates. This role is designed for institutions that have dedicated staff for payment plan operations separate from general accounts receivable.

Why: Institutions with high payment plan volume (typically those with a high percentage of first-generation or lower-income students) often have dedicated staff whose entire job is plan servicing. The Payment Plan Coordinator role lets NBS support those operational models without forcing coordinators to use overly broad Finance Admin access.

Read-Only Viewer. The Read-Only Viewer role provides view-only access to student accounts and active payment plans within the institution tenant. Viewers can see student balances, payment history, active plan details, and account notes. They cannot initiate any transactions, generate reports, access configuration, or view audit logs. This role is appropriate for enrollment management staff or financial aid advisors who need to verify a student's payment status before advising on financial aid or registration holds, but who have no operational function within Horizon.

Why: Cross-departmental visibility into student account status is a common institutional need — financial aid needs to know if a student is on a plan before advising on enrollment, and academic advisors need to know if a hold is payment-related before directing a student to appeal. Read-Only satisfies this without introducing operational risk.

NBS Internal Roles

NBS Implementation Engineer. The NBS Implementation Engineer role provides access to institution tenant configuration during the implementation phase. Implementation Engineers can create and modify payment plan templates, configure fee rules, set up ERP integration parameters, configure communication templates, and provision initial institution admin users. Implementation Engineer access to a tenant is time-bounded — it must be automatically restricted to read-only upon go-live, with Full Admin access requiring explicit re-authorization per incident. All Implementation Engineer actions must be stamped in the audit log as NBS-sourced with the specific NBS user's identity.

Why: Ongoing broad configuration access for implementation engineers after go-live is a security and compliance risk — institutions have contractual and regulatory expectations that NBS cannot silently modify their configuration. Time-bounding implementation access enforces a clean handoff to institution ownership and reduces the blast radius of any accidental or unauthorized configuration change post-launch.

NBS PAS Support Agent. The NBS PAS (Payment Administration Services) Support Agent role provides access to perform plan re-enrollment operations, process exceptions, and make manual adjustments on behalf of institutions that have contracted PAS services. PAS Agents can: view and enroll students in payment plans, process scheduled payments, handle NSF/return events, and add account notes. PAS Agents cannot: modify plan templates, issue refunds outside of returned payment reversals, access reporting, or modify institutional configuration. PAS Agent access is perpetual for institutions under a PAS contract but is institution-scoped — a PAS agent for Institution A cannot access Institution B.

Why: PAS is a managed service where NBS staff perform operational work on behalf of the institution. The role must be carefully scoped to exactly what PAS operations require — broad access would expose institutions to risk and erode trust in the PAS model as a managed service rather than full admin outsourcing.

NBS Technical Support. The NBS Technical Support role provides read-only access to student accounts and system logs for troubleshooting purposes. Technical Support cannot initiate transactions, modify configuration, or access audit log exports. Technical Support access to a tenant requires a logged support ticket reference — access must not be possible without a ticket number, and the ticket-access linkage must appear in the audit trail. This role is for diagnosing integration issues, payment processing failures, and data sync problems without operational capability.

Why: Technical support staff need to reproduce and diagnose issues, which requires seeing account state. However, giving support staff operational access creates risk of accidental or unauthorized changes. The ticket-linkage requirement creates accountability — every technical support access event is traceable to a specific institutional request.

Secured Actions & Permission Matrix

Cancel Active Payment Plan. Cancelling an active payment plan is a high-stakes irreversible action that immediately terminates scheduled payments, triggers cancellation communications, and may affect a student's registration hold status. This action requires: Finance Admin or Full Admin role (Student Services Rep and lower cannot cancel plans), a mandatory reason code selection from a configurable list, an optional free-text note, and a confirmation modal that surfaces the plan's remaining balance and the student's current hold status before execution. The cancel action must be logged in the audit trail with actor, timestamp, reason code, and the plan's state at time of cancellation.

Why: Plan cancellation errors are one of the most common complaint types NBS receives from institutions — a cancelled plan that should have been modified, or a plan cancelled on the wrong student account, causes significant downstream consequences (hold reinstated, student can't register, angry calls to bursar). The confirmation step and reason code requirement create a friction-and-documentation layer that catches most errors before they are committed.

Process Manual Refund. Issuing a refund to a student's payment method is restricted to Finance Admin and Full Admin roles. The refund workflow must: (1) require selection of the original transaction being refunded (refunds cannot be issued without a linked source transaction); (2) enforce refund amount ≤ original transaction amount; (3) require a reason code and optional note; (4) surface a confirmation step showing the student's name, refund amount, destination payment method, and estimated processing time; (5) log the full refund record in the audit trail. Refunds on payments older than the configured lookback window (default: 180 days) must require Full Admin approval.

Why: Refund fraud and error are high-frequency problems in student accounts receivable. Requiring transaction linkage prevents issuing refunds that don't correspond to real payments. The 180-day approval gate catches the edge case of a staff member trying to issue a refund on a payment that has already been reconciled and closed.

Student Account Impersonation / View As. NBS Technical Support and Implementation Engineers can activate a "View As" mode that renders the student-facing Horizon interface as it appears to a specific student, for troubleshooting and QA purposes. View As is strictly read-only — no transactions can be initiated while in View As mode. View As sessions must be logged with the NBS user's identity, the student account accessed, the ticket reference, and the session start and end timestamps. View As must not be available to any institution-side role at any permission level — institution staff must never be able to impersonate a student account.

Why: View As is the primary debugging tool for student-reported UI/UX issues that are difficult to reproduce in a test environment. Restricting it to NBS-only access with mandatory logging addresses both the FERPA concern (accessing a student's account view) and the operational risk (institution staff using View As to verify student credentials or view information beyond their authorized scope).

Batch Operations (Mass Enrollment, Mass Cancel, Mass Adjustment). Any operation that affects more than a single student account simultaneously — bulk plan enrollment from a file, mass plan cancellation for a term, or mass balance adjustment — is restricted to Full Admin role only and requires a two-step process: (1) upload and validate a batch file with a preview of affected accounts and a count of records; (2) explicit secondary confirmation acknowledging the scope of impact before execution. Batch operations must generate a results log downloadable after completion showing success/failure per record. Batch operations initiated outside of business hours require Full Admin authorization from a second Full Admin (four-eyes approval).

Why: Batch operation errors are catastrophic at scale — a misformatted file that cancels 2,000 plans instead of 200 is not recoverable without contacting every affected student. The preview-then-confirm pattern and four-eyes approval for after-hours operations are standard controls for high-impact bulk actions in financial systems. The results log is required for reconciliation and for support when students contact the bursar about unexpected changes.

Configuration Changes (Plan Templates, Fee Rules, Term Windows). Modifying institution-level configuration — payment plan templates, application fees, enrollment windows, auto-debit settings, or communication trigger settings — is restricted to Full Admin role only. Configuration changes must: display a summary of what is being changed (before/after) in a confirmation step; require a mandatory notes field explaining the reason for the change; and be logged in the audit trail with a before/after snapshot. Configuration changes that affect active plans (e.g., changing a payment schedule on a template that has enrolled students) must surface a warning listing the number of affected active plans before the change can be saved.

Why: Configuration changes have institution-wide impact and are a primary source of support escalations when they are made incorrectly or without understanding downstream effects. The before/after snapshot in the audit log is essential for troubleshooting — institutions that change something and see unexpected behavior need to be able to identify exactly what changed and when, without relying on NBS to reconstruct the configuration history.

User Provisioning (Add, Deactivate, Modify Institution Users). Managing institution admin users — adding new users, deactivating departing staff, or changing a user's assigned role — is restricted to Full Admin. User deactivation must be immediate: all active sessions for the deactivated user must be terminated within 60 seconds of the deactivation action, regardless of which device or browser is in use. New user invitations expire after 72 hours; expired invitations require a Full Admin to re-send. Role changes take effect on the user's next login. The user provisioning log (who was added/changed/deactivated, by whom, and when) must be included in the audit log export.

Why: Staff turnover at institutions is high and not always orderly — employees may leave without notice, and the window between departure and deactivation is a compliance and security risk. The 60-second session termination requirement means a Full Admin can immediately close access for a departed employee without waiting for a session timeout. The 72-hour invitation expiry prevents stale invitations accumulating for positions that were filled differently than anticipated.

ERP Integration Credentials & Sync Configuration. Access to view or modify ERP integration credentials, API keys, sync schedules, or field mapping configuration is restricted to Full Admin and NBS Implementation Engineer roles only. Credential values must be masked in the UI (show only last 4 characters); they cannot be retrieved in plaintext through any role, including Full Admin — only rotated. Any modification to sync configuration must require confirmation and must be logged in the audit trail. NBS Technical Support can view sync logs and error messages but cannot view credentials or modify sync configuration.

Why: ERP integration credentials are high-value targets — access to an institution's SIS API credential could expose the entire student record database, not just Horizon data. Masking and rotation-only access is standard credential hygiene. The restriction means even a compromised Full Admin session cannot exfiltrate the integration credential itself.

Financial Reporting & Data Export. Generating and exporting financial reports — particularly the audit log export, outstanding balance report, and term reconciliation report — requires at minimum Finance Admin or Reporting & Compliance Admin role. Reports containing individual student PII (name, ID, balance, payment history) must be logged when exported: who exported, which report, date range filtered, and timestamp. Reports with more than 500 student records must require a secondary confirmation acknowledging the scope of data being exported. Bulk exports must not be available via any publicly accessible or unauthenticated endpoint.

Why: FERPA requires institutions to track disclosures of student education records. A bulk export of student payment data is a FERPA disclosure event. Logging exports and requiring confirmation for large exports creates the documentation trail that institutions need to demonstrate FERPA compliance during audits and adds friction that deters casual over-exporting by staff who don't need bulk data for their role.

Financial Responsibility Agreement Version Management. Creating, publishing, and deprecating Financial Responsibility Agreement versions — including editing FRA content, setting effective dates, and configuring which student populations are required to complete the FRA before accessing Horizon — is restricted to Full Admin. FRA version changes require a before/after preview and mandatory confirmation. Once an FRA version has been signed by any student, it cannot be edited or deleted; a new version must be created. The FRA version history and signing records must be permanently retained (minimum 7 years) and must not be affected by tenant deactivation or data retention cleanup jobs.

Why: The FRA is the legal foundation of the institution's right to collect and to impose the fees and practices described in this PRD. An FRA that has been altered after student signature is a document integrity failure that could invalidate the institution's legal position in a collections dispute. Immutable version history and long-term retention are not feature preferences — they are legal requirements.

Collections Agency Referral. Referring a student account to an outside collections agency — or removing a student from collections referral — is restricted to Finance Admin and Full Admin. The referral workflow must require: selection of the agency from the institution's configured agencies list; confirmation of the balance being referred; documentation of prior contact attempts (as required by FDCPA); and a final confirmation showing the student's name, balance, and the disclosure that will be sent to the student notifying them of the referral. The referral event must be logged in the audit trail and must suppress all standard Horizon communications to that student account until the referral is resolved.

Why: Collections referral is a major escalation that has significant consequences for the student (credit impact, collection fees, potential legal action) and carries regulatory requirements for the institution. Restricting it to Finance Admin+ and requiring documentation of prior contact attempts is a FDCPA compliance requirement. The communication suppression after referral prevents the institution from inadvertently continuing to contact a student who has been referred, which has additional FDCPA implications.

User Management & Provisioning

Self-Service Institution User Management. Full Admins must be able to manage all institution user accounts — add, deactivate, modify role, reset password/invitation — without NBS involvement. The user management interface must show all active and inactive users, their assigned role, last login date, and any pending invitations. Bulk deactivation (multiple users at once) must be supported for use during term-end staff transitions. NBS must not be required for any routine user provisioning action, including initial setup beyond the first Full Admin account which NBS provisions during implementation.

Why: If every user add/change/deactivate requires an NBS support ticket, institutions will stop maintaining user hygiene — they'll leave departed employees active because it's too much friction to deactivate them. Self-service is both a product quality requirement and a security requirement.

Session Management & Security Policy. The platform must enforce configurable session timeout for all institution admin users (default: 30-minute idle timeout, Full Admin can configure from 15 to 120 minutes). All admin sessions must use secure, httpOnly cookies; session tokens must not be accessible via JavaScript. Concurrent session limits per user must be configurable (default: 3 simultaneous sessions). Failed login attempts must be rate-limited with lockout after a configurable number of failures (default: 5 attempts, 15-minute lockout). All session start, session end, and lockout events must appear in the audit log.

Why: Session security is a baseline compliance requirement for any financial platform. Idle timeout protects against unattended workstations in shared offices (common in bursar environments). Concurrent session limits detect shared credentials (a compliance violation). The audit log requirement for session events supports both internal security monitoring and external audit response.

Multi-Factor Authentication. MFA must be required for all institution admin logins. Supported MFA methods must include: TOTP authenticator app (Google Authenticator, Authy, Microsoft Authenticator), email OTP, and SMS OTP. Institutions using SSO for admin authentication must configure MFA at the IdP level — Horizon must enforce that the SSO authentication includes an MFA claim before granting access. Full Admin accounts must require MFA; lower roles may have MFA configurable as optional or required at the institution's discretion. MFA bypass codes (for account recovery) must be single-use and must be logged.

Why: Credential compromise is the most common attack vector for financial application breaches. MFA eliminates the risk from password reuse and phishing — both of which are common in higher education environments where staff use institutional email for personal communication. Requiring MFA for Full Admin accounts specifically addresses the highest-impact compromise scenario: an attacker with Full Admin credentials can modify configuration, export bulk student data, and issue refunds.

Direct Authentication for SSO Accounts

Institution Admin

Institution-Configurable Direct Auth Fallback. For institutions using SAML SSO, institution admins can configure whether direct authentication is enabled as a fallback when SSO is unavailable for a student (default: enabled). When enabled, students whose SSO authentication fails are presented with a "Can't access your institution account?" option that initiates the direct auth flow.

Why: Students who have graduated, taken leaves of absence, or whose institutional accounts have been deactivated lose SSO access while active payment obligations remain; without a fallback, these students cannot manage their accounts or make payments without calling support.

Student / Consumer

Knowledge-Based Direct Authentication. Direct auth requires the student to provide their email address on file plus one knowledge-based verification factor. The institution configures which factor is required: date of birth or last 4 digits of SSN. The student cannot choose between factors; the institution's configured factor is the only option presented.

Why: A two-factor verification (email + knowledge factor) provides a proportionate identity assurance level for read and payment-method update access; limiting to the institution-configured factor ensures consistency with the institution's student identity verification policy.

Student / Consumer

Restricted Direct Auth Session Scope. A session initiated via direct authentication provides read-only access to the student's balance and active plans, the ability to update their payment method, and access to their communication history. Direct auth sessions cannot be used to change account settings or enroll in new Pay Over Time plans. Sessions are time-limited to 24 hours and are not renewable within the same direct auth event.

Why: Limiting direct auth scope to payment management reduces the blast radius of a compromised direct auth session; enrollment restriction prevents new financial commitments from being made outside the institution's standard identity verification channel.

Institution Admin / Compliance

Direct Auth Audit Logging and Threshold Alerting. All direct auth sessions are logged in the access audit trail with a distinct session type marker differentiating them from SSO-authenticated sessions. When a student uses direct auth more than N times within a rolling 30-day window, the institution receives an automated notification. Institution admins configure N; the default threshold is 3.

Why: Repeated direct auth usage may indicate a student whose SSO account is permanently inaccessible and who needs proactive outreach, or may signal an account-takeover pattern that warrants investigation; the threshold alert surfaces either case without requiring manual audit log review.

Platform Requirements

Correspondence

Outbound communication infrastructure — email, in-app notifications, and SMS — covering template management, triggering logic, regulatory compliance, and institution customization.

Communication Infrastructure

Multi-Channel Delivery. The platform must support outbound communication via email (primary), in-app notifications (for authenticated users), and SMS (optional, institution-configurable). All channels must use a shared triggering engine so that an event (e.g., payment plan enrolled, payment failed) generates the appropriate notification across all enabled channels without duplicate logic per channel.

Why: Students consume information differently — many miss emails but respond to SMS. Institutions that can only communicate via email will see higher delinquency rates than institutions with multi-channel reach. A shared triggering engine prevents the nightmare of channel-specific bugs where email fires but SMS doesn't, or vice versa.

As an institution admin

Template Customization. Institution admins must be able to edit the body, subject line, and sender display name of any correspondence template for their tenant. Customizable elements must include: institution logo and branding, contact information, policy-specific language (e.g., late fee amounts, collection agency disclosure), and call-to-action text. A preview mode must allow admins to see a rendered version of any template before saving changes. Edits must not break the underlying event variable substitution (student name, amount, due date, etc.).

Why: Institutions have Financial Responsibility Agreements and legal disclosures that must appear in specific communications. Without template editing, NBS becomes a bottleneck for any institution that needs to update their collection disclosure language or rebrand after a name change.

Trigger Logic & Timing

Configurable Trigger Points. The following events must trigger outbound correspondence (configurable on/off per institution): (1) Payment plan enrollment confirmation; (2) Upcoming payment reminder (N days before due, configurable); (3) Payment processed confirmation; (4) Payment failed notification; (5) Payment plan default warning (after N failed payments); (6) Payment plan terminated notice; (7) Balance increase on active plan; (8) Plan approaching end; (9) Outstanding balance reminder (for accounts without a plan); (10) Authorized party invitation; (11) Custom plan issued by admin.

Why: Each of these moments is a consumer experience inflection point. Institutions that can tune their communication cadence to their student population will see better outcomes than those using a fixed NBS-defaults schedule. Correctness at each trigger point also has regulatory implications — failure to send a required default notice, for example, can affect the institution's ability to collect.

Communication Suppression for Enrolled Plans. When a student is actively enrolled in a payment plan that covers their outstanding balance, general outstanding balance reminder communications must be suppressed. The student is already in a remediation path — sending "your balance is past due" communications alongside the payment plan communications creates confusion and damages trust.

Why: This is one of the most common complaints NCC receives from institutions whose prior payment plan software continued sending dunning emails to students who were on a plan. Suppression logic requires the correspondence engine to be aware of the student's plan enrollment state, not just their account balance.

Regulatory & Compliance

TCPA Compliance for SMS. SMS communications must require explicit student opt-in prior to sending. Opt-in language must comply with TCPA and must be distinct from the general Financial Responsibility Agreement signature. Students must be able to opt out via reply STOP at any time. Opt-out must be processed within one message of receipt. The platform must maintain a log of opt-in/opt-out events for each student and phone number.

Why: TCPA violations carry statutory damages of $500–$1,500 per message. In a class action context — which is the typical enforcement vehicle — this becomes catastrophic. This is a hard compliance requirement, not a preference.

FDCPA-Compliant Collection Notices. Correspondence related to past-due balances that are being or may be referred to collections must comply with FDCPA requirements, including the 30-day validation notice, cessation of collection contact upon dispute, and identifying Horizon or NBS as the entity communicating on behalf of the creditor institution. The legal review process for collection-adjacent templates must involve compliance and legal, not just product.

Why: Higher education debt collection is under increasing regulatory scrutiny from the CFPB. Correspondence that violates FDCPA — even when sent by a software platform on behalf of an institution — can expose NBS to third-party collector liability if regulators determine Horizon is acting as a debt collector rather than a service provider.

Deep Links in Communications

Student / Consumer

Context-Specific Deep Link Routing. Every automated email and SMS notification sent by the platform must include a deep link routing the consumer directly to the relevant context in the portal rather than to the portal home screen. Required deep link destinations include: the specific plan detail view (plan confirmation email), the balance-change alert card (new charge alert), the update-payment-method flow for the affected plan (failed payment notification), and the upcoming installment detail for the affected plan (advance payment reminder).

Why: Routing consumers to the portal home screen forces unnecessary navigation at moments of urgency — a failed payment or new charge — increasing abandonment and support call volume. A direct deep link converts the notification from an alert into an actionable entry point.

Student / Consumer

Signed Token Authentication for Deep Links. Deep links carry a short-lived signed token that expires 72 hours after the notification is sent. For consumers who are already authenticated, the token auto-authenticates the session and navigates directly to the destination. For consumers who are not authenticated, the token routes them to an identity verification step (last 4 SSN or date of birth, per institution configuration) before activating the deep link destination.

Why: A signed, time-limited token prevents unauthorized access via forwarded or guessed links while removing login friction for consumers who click promptly; identity verification provides a proportionate fallback for unauthenticated access.

Institution Admin

Per-Template Deep Link Configuration. Institution admins can configure whether deep links are included for each individual notification template. An institution may disable deep links in specific templates (e.g., for compliance or accessibility tooling reasons) without affecting other templates.

Why: Some institutions have legal or accessibility requirements governing external links in student communications; per-template control allows targeted compliance without disabling the feature globally.

Platform Requirements

ERP Integration

Integration requirements for connecting Horizon to institution SIS/ERP systems. Covers supported platforms, data synchronization patterns, charge import, payment write-back, and enrollment status feeds.

Supported ERP Platforms

Tier 1 Certified Integrations. The platform must maintain certified, actively maintained integrations with the following Tier 1 higher education SIS/ERP platforms: Ellucian Banner (REST API and Ethos), Ellucian Colleague (REST and Ethos), Oracle PeopleSoft Campus Solutions, Workday Student, and Anthology Student (formerly Campus Management). Certified integrations must be regression-tested against platform updates and must be explicitly documented in the NCC integration certification matrix used by institutions evaluating vendor selection.

Why: These five platforms represent the SIS footprint of the overwhelming majority of NCC clients. Certification on these platforms is a table-stakes requirement for appearing on post-SIS-migration vendor shortlists, which is the primary acquisition vector identified in the HE Market Context section.

Tier 2 Partner Integrations. The platform must provide a documented, supported integration path for Tier 2 platforms including Jenzabar, Unit4, Salesforce Education Cloud, and custom/homegrown SIS systems. Tier 2 integrations must use a standardized data interchange format (documented API or file format) so that implementation cost and risk are bounded. Tier 2 integrations are not regression-tested by NBS but must be supportable by the technical support team.

Why: Institutions on non-Tier-1 platforms are often smaller or community colleges — the exact market segment where NCC is prioritizing growth. Providing a Tier 2 path ensures these institutions can be onboarded without a custom engineering engagement every time.

Data Sync Patterns

Charge & Balance Import. Horizon must import student charge and balance data from the institution's ERP on a configurable schedule (real-time via webhook/event, near-real-time batch ≤15 min, or nightly batch).
